<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/20px-Emblem-money.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/40px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Business" title="Portal:Business">Business&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Business_administration" title="Template:Business administration"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Business_administration" title="Template talk:Business administration"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Business_administration" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Business administration"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Management</b> (or <b>managing</b>) is the administration of organizations, whether <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">businesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_organization" title="Nonprofit organization">nonprofit organizations</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Government_agency" title="Government agency">government bodies</a> through <a href="/wiki/Business_administration" title="Business administration">business administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit_studies" title="Nonprofit studies">nonprofit management</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a> sub-field of <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> respectively. It is the process of managing the resources of businesses, governments, and other organizations.
</p><p>Larger organizations generally have three <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchical</a> levels of managers,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> organized in a pyramid structure:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Senior_management" title="Senior management">Senior management</a> roles include the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">board of directors</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a> (CEO) or a <a href="/wiki/President_(corporate_title)" title="President (corporate title)">president</a> of an organization. They set the strategic goals and policy of the organization and make decisions on how the overall organization will operate. Senior managers are generally executive-level professionals who provide direction to middle management. Compare <a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">governance</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Middle_management" title="Middle management">Middle management</a> roles include branch managers, regional managers, department managers, and section managers. They provide direction to front-line managers and communicate the strategic goals and policies of senior management to them.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Line_management" title="Line management">Line management</a> roles include <a href="/wiki/Supervisor" title="Supervisor">supervisors</a> and the frontline managers or <a href="/wiki/Team_leader" title="Team leader">team leaders</a> who oversee the work of regular employees, or volunteers in some voluntary organizations, and provide direction on their work. Line managers often perform the managerial functions that are traditionally considered the core of management. Despite the name, they are usually considered part of the workforce and not part of the organization's management class.</li></ul>

<span class="nowrap"><span class="mw-image-border noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/20px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/40px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231" /><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_sidebar" title="Template:Politics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Politics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Politics_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Politics sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Management is taught - both as a theoretical subject as well as a practical application - across different disciplines at colleges and universities. Prominent major degree-programs in management include <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Management" title="Doctor of Management">Management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Business_Administration" title="Bachelor of Business Administration">Business Administration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">Public Administration</a>. <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">Social scientists</a> study management as an <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a>, investigating areas such as <a href="/wiki/Social_organization" title="Social organization">social organization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organizational_adaptation" title="Organizational adaptation">organizational adaptation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organizational_leadership" class="mw-redirect" title="Organizational leadership">organizational leadership</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In recent decades, there has been a movement for <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_management" title="Evidence-based management">evidence-based management</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The English verb <i>manage</i> has its roots in the fifteenth-century <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> verb <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">mesnager</i></span>, which often referred in <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> language "to hold in hand the reins of a horse".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Also the <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> term <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">maneggiare</i></span> (to handle, especially tools or a horse) is possible. In <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">manejar</i></span> can also mean to rule the horses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These three terms derive from the two <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> words <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">manus</i></span> (hand) and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">agere</i></span> (to act). 
</p><p>The word <i>management</i> dates back to the 1590s, when it was first used to mean "the act of managing by direction or manipulation," formed from manage plus the suffix -ment. By the 1670s, it had also come to describe "the act of managing by physical manipulation." Later, in 1739, the word became increasingly used to refer to "a governing body" or "the directors of an undertaking collectively," a sense that originally applied to theaters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definitions">Definitions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Views on the definition and scope of management include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Henri_Fayol" title="Henri Fayol">Henri Fayol</a> (1841–1925) stated: "To manage is to forecast and to plan, to organize, to command, to co-ordinate and to control".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gulshan&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gulshan-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fredmund_Malik" title="Fredmund Malik">Fredmund Malik</a> (born 1944) defines management as "the transformation of resources into utility".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ghislain_Deslandes" title="Ghislain Deslandes">Ghislain Deslandes</a> defines management as "a vulnerable force, under pressure to achieve results and endowed with the triple power of constraint, imitation, and imagination, operating on subjective, <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">interpersonal</a>, institutional and environmental levels".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Drucker" title="Peter Drucker">Peter Drucker</a> (1909–2005) saw the basic task of management as twofold: <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Theoretical_scope">Theoretical scope</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Theoretical scope"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Management involves identifying the <a href="/wiki/Mission_statement" title="Mission statement">mission</a>, objective, <a href="/wiki/Procedure_(business)" title="Procedure (business)">procedures</a>, rules and manipulation<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">enterprise</a> to contribute to the success of the enterprise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scholars have focused on the management of individual,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> organizational,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and inter-organizational relationships. This implies effective <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>: an enterprise environment (as opposed to a physical or mechanical mechanism) implies human <a href="/wiki/Motivation" title="Motivation">motivation</a> and implies some sort of successful progress or <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">system</a> outcome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As such, management is not the manipulation of a mechanism (machine or automated program), not the herding of animals, and can occur either in a legal or in an illegal enterprise or environment. From an individual's perspective, management does not need to be seen solely from an enterprise point of view, because management is a function in improving one's <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Social relationship">relationships</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Management is seen in various parts of society.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>Plans, <a href="/wiki/Measurements" class="mw-redirect" title="Measurements">measurements</a>, motivational <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> tools, goals, and economic measures (profit, etc.) may or may not be necessary components for there to be management. At first, one views management functionally, such as measuring quantity, adjusting <a href="/wiki/Plan" title="Plan">plans</a>, and meeting <a href="/wiki/Goal" title="Goal">goals</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but this applies even in situations where planning does not take place. From this perspective, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Fayol" title="Henri Fayol">Henri Fayol</a> (1841–1925)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> considers management to consist of five <a href="/wiki/Function_(engineering)" title="Function (engineering)">functions</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>planning (forecasting)</li>
<li>organizing</li>
<li>commanding</li>
<li>coordinating</li>
<li>controlling</li></ul>
<p>In another way of thinking, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Parker_Follett" title="Mary Parker Follett">Mary Parker Follett</a> (1868–1933), allegedly defined management as "the art of getting things done through people".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> She described management as a philosophy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some scholars however find this definition useful but far too narrow. The phrase "management is what managers do" occurs widely,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suggesting the difficulty of defining management without <a href="/wiki/Circular_definition" title="Circular definition">circularity</a>, the shifting nature of definitions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and the connection of <a href="/wiki/Managerialism" title="Managerialism">managerial practices</a> with the existence of a <a href="/wiki/Managerial_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Managerial class">managerial cadre</a> or of a <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">class</a>.
</p><p>One habit of thought regards management as equivalent to "<a href="/wiki/Business_administration" title="Business administration">business administration</a>" and thus excludes management in places outside <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>, for example in <a href="/wiki/Charitable_organization" title="Charitable organization">charities</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a>. More broadly, every organization must "manage" its work, people, processes, technology, etc. to maximize effectiveness.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Nonetheless, many people refer to university departments that teach management as "<a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">business schools</a>". Some such institutions (such as the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a>) use that name, while others (such as the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Management" title="Yale School of Management">Yale School of Management</a>) employ the broader term "management".
</p><p>English speakers may also use the term "management" or "the management" as a collective word describing the managers of an organization, for example of a <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Historically this use of the term often contrasted with the term <i><a href="/wiki/Industrial_relations" title="Industrial relations">labor</a></i> – referring to those being managed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Levels">Levels</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Levels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:USCG_Org_Chart.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/USCG_Org_Chart.jpg/250px-USCG_Org_Chart.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="271" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/USCG_Org_Chart.jpg/429px-USCG_Org_Chart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/USCG_Org_Chart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="506" /></a><figcaption>An <a href="/wiki/Organization_chart" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization chart">organization chart</a> for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a> shows the hierarchy of managerial roles in that organization.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Top_management">Top management</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Top management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The board of directors is typically primarily composed of non-executives who owe a <a href="/wiki/Fiduciary" title="Fiduciary">fiduciary</a> duty to shareholders and are not closely involved in the day-to-day activities of the organization. However, this varies depending on the type (e.g., public versus private), size, and culture of the organization. These directors are theoretically liable for breaches of that duty and are typically insured under <a href="/wiki/Directors_and_officers_liability_insurance" title="Directors and officers liability insurance">directors and officers liability insurance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> directors are estimated to spend 4.4 hours per week on board duties, and median compensation was $212,512 in 2010. The board sets corporate strategy, makes major decisions such as major acquisitions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and hires, evaluates, and fires the top-level manager (<a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a> or CEO). The CEO typically hires other positions. However, board involvement in the hiring of other positions such as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_financial_officer" title="Chief financial officer">chief financial officer</a> (CFO) has increased.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2013, a survey of over 160 CEOs and directors of public and private companies found that the top weaknesses of CEOs were "<a href="/wiki/Mentoring" class="mw-redirect" title="Mentoring">mentoring</a> skills" and "board engagement", and 10% of companies never evaluated the CEO.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The board may also have certain employees (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Internal_auditor" title="Internal auditor">internal auditors</a>) report to them or directly hire independent <a href="/wiki/General_contractor" title="General contractor">contractors</a>; for example, the board (through the <a href="/wiki/Audit_committee" title="Audit committee">audit committee</a>) typically selects the <a href="/wiki/Auditor" title="Auditor">auditor</a>.
</p><p>Helpful skills for top management vary by the type of organization but typically include a broad understanding of competition, world economies, effective planning, and politics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, the CEO is responsible for implementing and determining (within the board's framework) the broad policies of the organization. Executive management accomplishes the day-to-day details, including instructions for the preparation of department budgets, procedures, and schedules; appointment of middle-level executives such as department managers; coordination of departments; media and governmental relations; and <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholder</a> communication.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Line_management">Line management</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Line management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Line managers include <a href="/wiki/Supervisor" title="Supervisor">supervisors</a>, section leaders, forepersons, and team leaders. They focus on controlling and directing regular employees, either in direct service delivery or in <a href="/wiki/Back_office" title="Back office">back-office</a> areas of work. They are usually responsible for assigning employees tasks, guiding and supervising employees on day-to-day activities, ensuring the quality and quantity of production and/or service, making recommendations and suggestions to employees on their work, and channeling employee concerns that they cannot resolve to mid-level managers or other administrators. Low-level, frontline or "front-line" managers also act as <a href="/wiki/Role_model" title="Role model">role models</a> for their team members. Deficits in frontline management can impact critically on service delivery and customer satisfaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Training_and_education">Training and education</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Training and education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">Business school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_policy_school" title="Public policy school">Public policy school</a>, and <a href="/wiki/College_of_Arts_and_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="College of Arts and Sciences">College of Arts and Sciences</a></div>
<p>Colleges and universities worldwide offer bachelor's degrees, graduate programs, diplomas, and professional certificates in management. These are most commonly housed within colleges of business, business schools, or faculties of management, but may also be offered in related departments such as economics, public policy, or the social sciences. 
</p><p>Scholars have argued that higher education played a central role in the so-called "managerial revolution" of the 20th century, by formalizing managerial skills and expanding the professionalization of management as a discipline.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Undergraduate">Undergraduate</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Undergraduate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Business_education#Undergraduate_education" title="Business education">Business education §&#160;Undergraduate education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">Political science</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">Public administration</a></div>
<p>At the undergraduate level, the most common business programs are the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Business_Administration" title="Bachelor of Business Administration">Bachelor of Business Administration</a> (BBA) and <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Commerce" title="Bachelor of Commerce">Bachelor of Commerce</a> (B.Com.).
These typically comprise a four-year program designed to give students an overview of the role of managers in planning and directing within an organization. 
Course topics include accounting, financial management, statistics, marketing, strategy, and other related areas.
</p><p>Many other undergraduate degrees include the study of management, such as <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">Bachelor of Science</a> degrees with a major in <a href="/wiki/Business_administration" title="Business administration">business administration</a> or management and the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> (BA) or <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">Bachelor of Science</a> (BS) in <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a> (PoliSci) with a concentration in <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> or the Bachelor of Public Administration (B.P.A), a degree designed for individuals aiming to work as <a href="/wiki/Bureaucrat" title="Bureaucrat">bureaucrats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">government jobs</a>. 
Many colleges and universities also offer certificates and diplomas in business administration or management, which typically require one to two years of full-time study.
</p><p>To manage technological areas, one often needs an undergraduate degree in a <a href="/wiki/Science,_technology,_engineering,_and_mathematics#Immigration_policy" title="Science, technology, engineering, and mathematics">STEM area</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Graduate">Graduate</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Graduate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Business_education#Postgraduate_education" title="Business education">Business education §&#160;Postgraduate education</a></div>
<p>At the graduate level students aiming at careers as managers or executives may choose to specialize in major subareas of management or business administration such as <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_resources" title="Human resources">human resources</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_business" title="International business">international business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organizational_behavior" title="Organizational behavior">organizational behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organizational_theory" title="Organizational theory">organizational theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strategic_management" title="Strategic management">strategic management</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Accounting" title="Accounting">accounting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_finance" title="Corporate finance">corporate finance</a>, entertainment, global management, <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare management">healthcare management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Investment_management" title="Investment management">investment management</a>, sustainability and <a href="/wiki/Real_estate" title="Real estate">real estate</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Good_practices">Good practices</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Good practices"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>While management trends can change fast, the long-term trend in management has been defined by a market embracing diversity and a rising service industry. Managers are currently being trained to encourage greater <a href="/wiki/Equality_of_opportunities" class="mw-redirect" title="Equality of opportunities">equality of opportunities</a> for minorities and women in the workplace, offering increased flexibility in working hours, better retraining, and innovative (and usually industry-specific) performance markers. Managers destined for the service sector are being trained to use unique measurement techniques, better worker support, and more charismatic leadership styles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Promotion_(rank)" title="Promotion (rank)">Promotion</a> prospects can <a href="/wiki/Incentive" title="Incentive">incentivise</a> performance improvements.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-j780&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-j780-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_resources" title="Human resources">Human resources</a> finds itself increasingly working with management in a training capacity to help collect management data on the success (or failure) of management actions with employees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Good practices identified for managers include "walking the shop floor",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and, especially for managers who are new in post, identifying and achieving some "quick wins" which demonstrate visible success in establishing appropriate objectives. Leadership writer <a href="/wiki/John_Kotter" title="John Kotter">John Kotter</a> uses the phrase "Short-Term Wins" to express the same idea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As in all work, achieving an appropriate <a href="/wiki/Work-life_balance" class="mw-redirect" title="Work-life balance">work-life balance</a> for self and others is an important management practice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Evidence-based_management">Evidence-based management</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Evidence-based management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_management" title="Evidence-based management">Evidence-based management</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_management" title="Evidence-based management">Evidence-based management</a> is an emerging movement to use the current, best evidence in management and <a href="/wiki/Decision-making" title="Decision-making">decision-making</a>. It is part of the larger movement towards <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Evidence-based practices">evidence-based practices</a>. Evidence-based management entails managerial decisions and organizational practices informed by the best available evidence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pfeffer&#95;2006&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pfeffer_2006-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As with other evidence-based practice, this is based on the three principles of published peer-reviewed (often in management or social science journals) research evidence that bears on whether and why a particular management practice works; judgment and experience from contextual management practice, to understand the organization and interpersonal dynamics in a situation and determine the risks and benefits of available actions; and the preferences and values of those affected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some see management as a late-modern (in the sense of late <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>) conceptualization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the changing workplaces of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> in the 18th and 19th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> theory and practice contributed approaches to managing the newly popular <a href="/wiki/Factory" title="Factory">factories</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Early_writing">Early writing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Early writing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Written in 1776 by <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">moral philosopher</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i> discussed efficient organization of work through <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-management&#95;gomez-mejia&#95;p20&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-management_gomez-mejia_p20-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Smith described how changes in processes could boost productivity in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Pin_(device)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pin (device)">pins</a>. While individuals could produce 200 pins per day, Smith analyzed the steps involved in the manufacture and, with 10 specialists, enabled the production of 48,000 pins per day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-management&#95;gomez-mejia&#95;p20&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-management_gomez-mejia_p20-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="19th_century">19th century</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 19th century"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Classical economists such as <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> (1723–1790) and <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> (1806–1873) provided a theoretical background to <a href="/wiki/Resource_allocation" title="Resource allocation">resource allocation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Production_(economics)" title="Production (economics)">production (economics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pricing" title="Pricing">pricing</a> issues. About the same time, innovators like <a href="/wiki/Eli_Whitney" title="Eli Whitney">Eli Whitney</a> (1765–1825), <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a> (1736–1819), and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Boulton" title="Matthew Boulton">Matthew Boulton</a> (1728–1809) developed elements of technical production such as <a href="/wiki/Standardization" title="Standardization">standardization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quality_control" title="Quality control">quality-control</a> procedures, <a href="/wiki/Cost_accounting" title="Cost accounting">cost-accounting</a>, interchangeability of parts, and <a href="/wiki/Plan" title="Plan">work-planning</a>. Many of these aspects of management existed in the pre-1861 slave-based sector of the US economy. That environment saw 4 million people, as the contemporary usages had it, "managed" in profitable quasi-<a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
before <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage slavery</a> eclipsed chattel slavery.
</p><p>Salaried managers as an identifiable group first became prominent in the late 19th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As large corporations began to overshadow small family businesses the need for personnel management positions became more necessary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Businesses grew into large corporations and the need for clerks, bookkeepers, secretaries and managers expanded. The demand for trained managers led college and university administrators to consider and move forward with plans to create the first schools of business on their campuses.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="20th_century">20th century</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 20th century"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredrick_Winslow_Taylor_c1907_retouched.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Fredrick_Winslow_Taylor_c1907_retouched.png/250px-Fredrick_Winslow_Taylor_c1907_retouched.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="342" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Fredrick_Winslow_Taylor_c1907_retouched.png/500px-Fredrick_Winslow_Taylor_c1907_retouched.png 1.5x" data-file-width="926" data-file-height="1265" /></a><figcaption>Frederick Winslow Taylor <i>circa</i> 1907</figcaption></figure>
<p>At the turn of the twentieth century, the need for skilled and trained managers had become increasingly apparent.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The demand occurred as personnel departments began to expand rapidly. In 1915, less than one in twenty manufacturing firms had a dedicated personnel department. By 1929 that number had grown to over one-third.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Formal management education became standardized at colleges and universities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Colleges and universities capitalized on the needs of corporations by forming business schools and corporate-placement departments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This shift toward formal business education marked the creation of a corporate élite in the US.
</p><p>By about 1900 one finds managers trying to place their theories on what they regarded as a thoroughly scientific basis (see <a href="/wiki/Scientism" title="Scientism">scientism</a> for perceived limitations of this belief). Examples include <a href="/wiki/Henry_R._Towne" title="Henry R. Towne">Henry R. Towne</a>'s <i>Science of management</i> in the 1890s, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Winslow_Taylor" title="Frederick Winslow Taylor">Frederick Winslow Taylor</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Principles_of_Scientific_Management" title="The Principles of Scientific Management">The Principles of Scientific Management</a></i> (1911), <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Moller_Gilbreth" title="Lillian Moller Gilbreth">Lillian Gilbreth</a>'s <i>Psychology of Management</i> (1914),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bunker_Gilbreth" title="Frank Bunker Gilbreth">Frank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Moller_Gilbreth" title="Lillian Moller Gilbreth">Lillian Gilbreth</a>'s <i>Applied motion study</i> (1917), and <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Gantt" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry L. Gantt">Henry L. Gantt</a>'s charts (1910s). J. Duncan wrote the first <a href="/wiki/College" title="College">college</a> management <a href="/wiki/Textbook" title="Textbook">textbook</a> in 1911. In 1912 <a href="/wiki/Yoichi_Ueno" title="Yoichi Ueno">Yoichi Ueno</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Taylorism" class="mw-redirect" title="Taylorism">Taylorism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and became the first <a href="/wiki/Management_consultant" class="mw-redirect" title="Management consultant">management consultant</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_management_culture" title="Japanese management culture"> "Japanese management style"</a>. His son Ichiro Ueno pioneered Japanese <a href="/wiki/Quality_assurance" title="Quality assurance">quality assurance</a>.
</p><p>The first comprehensive theories of management appeared around 1920.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a> offered the first <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Business_Administration" title="Master of Business Administration">Master of Business Administration</a> degree (MBA) in 1921. People like <a href="/wiki/Henri_Fayol" title="Henri Fayol">Henri Fayol</a> (1841–1925) and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_Church" title="Alexander Hamilton Church">Alexander Church</a> (1866–1936) described the various branches of management and their inter-relationships. In the early 20th century, people like Ordway Tead (1891–1973), <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dill_Scott" title="Walter Dill Scott">Walter Scott</a> (1869–1955) and J. Mooney applied the principles of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> to management. Other writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Elton_Mayo" title="Elton Mayo">Elton Mayo</a> (1880–1949), <a href="/wiki/Mary_Follett" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Follett">Mary Parker Follett</a> (1868–1933), <a href="/wiki/Chester_Barnard" title="Chester Barnard">Chester Barnard</a> (1886–1961), <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> (1864–1920, who saw what he called the "administrator" as <a href="/wiki/Bureaucrat" title="Bureaucrat">bureaucrat</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Rensis_Likert" title="Rensis Likert">Rensis Likert</a> (1903–1981), and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Argyris" title="Chris Argyris">Chris Argyris</a> (born 1923) approached the phenomenon of management from a <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociological</a> perspective.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Drucker" title="Peter Drucker">Peter Drucker</a> (1909–2005) wrote one of the earliest books on applied management: <i><a href="/wiki/Concept_of_the_Corporation" title="Concept of the Corporation">Concept of the Corporation</a></i> (published in 1946). It resulted from <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Sloan" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Sloan">Alfred Sloan</a> (chairman of <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Corporation">General Motors</a> until 1956) commissioning a study of the <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">organization</a>. Drucker went on to write 39 books, many in the same vein.
</p><p>H. Dodge, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fisher" title="Ronald Fisher">Ronald Fisher</a> (1890–1962), and Thornton C. Fry introduced statistical techniques into management studies. In the 1940s, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Maynard_Stuart_Blackett" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrick Maynard Stuart Blackett">Patrick Blackett</a> worked in the development of the <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied-mathematics</a> <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> of <a href="/wiki/Operations_research" title="Operations research">operations research</a>, initially for military operations. Operations research, sometimes known as "<a href="/wiki/Management_science" title="Management science">management science</a>" (but distinct from Taylor's <a href="/wiki/Scientific_management" title="Scientific management">scientific management</a>), attempts to take a <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> approach to solving decision-problems and can apply directly to multiple management problems, particularly in the areas of <a href="/wiki/Logistics" title="Logistics">logistics</a> and operations.
</p><p>Some of the later 20th-century developments include the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_constraints" title="Theory of constraints">theory of constraints</a> (introduced in 1984), <a href="/wiki/Management_by_objectives" title="Management by objectives">management by objectives</a> (systematized in 1954), the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Harzburg_Model&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Harzburg Model (page does not exist)">Harzburg Model</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harzburger_Modell" class="extiw" title="de:Harzburger Modell">de</a>&#93;</span><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
(developed by <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_H%C3%B6hn" title="Reinhard Höhn">Reinhard Höhn</a> in post-war Germany), <a href="/wiki/Business_process_reengineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Business process reengineering">re-engineering</a> (the early 1990s), <a href="/wiki/Six_Sigma" title="Six Sigma">Six Sigma</a> (1986), <a href="/wiki/Management_by_walking_around" class="mw-redirect" title="Management by walking around">management by walking around</a> (1970s), the <a href="/wiki/Viable_system_model" title="Viable system model">Viable system model</a> (1972), and various <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology"> information-technology</a>-driven theories such as <a href="/wiki/Agile_software_development" title="Agile software development">agile software development</a> (so-named from 2001), as well as group-management theories such as <a href="/wiki/Cog%27s_Ladder" class="mw-redirect" title="Cog&#39;s Ladder">Cog's Ladder</a> (1972) and the notion of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Peters" title="Tom Peters">"thriving on chaos"</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (1987).
</p><p>As the general recognition of managers as a class solidified during the 20th century and gave perceived practitioners of the art/science of management a certain amount of prestige, so the way opened for <a href="/wiki/Business_philosophies_and_popular_management_theories" class="mw-redirect" title="Business philosophies and popular management theories">popularised systems of management ideas</a> to peddle their wares. In this context, many <a href="/wiki/Management_fad" title="Management fad">management fads</a> may have had more to do with <a href="/wiki/Pop_psychology" class="mw-redirect" title="Pop psychology">pop psychology</a> than with scientific theories of management.
</p><p>Business management includes the following branches:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Finance" title="Finance">financial management</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Human_resource_management" title="Human resource management">human resource management</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Management_cybernetics" title="Management cybernetics">management cybernetics</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Information_technology_management" title="Information technology management">information technology management</a> (responsible for <a href="/wiki/Management_information_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Management information systems">management information systems</a> )</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marketing_management" title="Marketing management">marketing management</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Operations_management" title="Operations management">operations management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">production</a> management</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Strategic_management" title="Strategic management">strategic management</a></li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="21st_century">21st century</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 21st century"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Branches of management theory also exist relating to <a href="/wiki/Non-profit_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-profit organization">nonprofits</a> and to government: such as <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Public management">public management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Educational_management" title="Educational management">educational management</a>. Further, management programs related to <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> organizations have also spawned programs in nonprofit management and <a href="/wiki/Social_entrepreneurship" title="Social entrepreneurship">social entrepreneurship</a>.
</p><p>Many of the assumptions made by management have come under attack from <a href="/wiki/Business_ethics" title="Business ethics">business-ethics</a> viewpoints, <a href="/wiki/Critical_management_studies" title="Critical management studies">critical management studies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-corporate_activism" title="Anti-corporate activism">anti-corporate activism</a>. This could include violations to a company’s <a href="/wiki/Ethics_policy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethics policy">ethics policy.</a> 
</p><p>As one consequence, <a href="/wiki/Workplace_democracy" title="Workplace democracy">workplace democracy</a> (sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_self-management" title="Workers&#39; self-management">Workers' self-management</a>) has become both more common and more advocated, in some places distributing all management functions among workers, each of whom takes on a portion of the work. However, these models predate any current political issue and may occur more naturally than does a <a href="/wiki/Command_hierarchy" title="Command hierarchy">command hierarchy</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Nature_of_work">Nature of work</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nature of work"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In profitable organizations, management's primary function is the satisfaction of a range of <a href="/wiki/Stakeholder_(corporate)" title="Stakeholder (corporate)">stakeholders</a>. This typically involves making a profit (for the shareholders), creating valued products at a reasonable cost (for customers), and providing great employment opportunities for employees. In case of nonprofit management, one of the main functions is, keeping the faith of donors. In most models of management and <a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">governance</a>, shareholders vote for the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">board of directors</a>, and the board then hires senior management. Some organizations have experimented with other methods (such as employee-voting models) of selecting or reviewing managers, but this is rare.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Topics">Topics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Topics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Basics">Basics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Management&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Basics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Henri_Fayol" title="Henri Fayol">Fayol</a>, management operates through five basic functions: planning, organizing, commanding, coordinating and controlling.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Planning</b>: Deciding what needs to happen in the future and generating action plans (deciding in advance).</li>
<li><b>Organizing</b> (or staffing): Making sure the human and nonhuman resources are put into place.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peaucelle2015&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peaucelle2015-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Commanding</b> (or leading): Determining what must be done in a situation and getting people to do it.</li>
<li><b>Coordinating</b>: Creating a structure through which an organization's goals can be accomplished.</li>
<li><b>Controlling</b>: Checking progress against plans.</li></ul>

Prabbal Frank attempts to make a subtle distinction between management and manipulation: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFFrank2007" class="citation book cs1">Frank, Prabbal (2007). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=H9n2vQ7JFVwC"><i>People Manipulation: A Positive Approach</i></a> (2&#160;ed.). New Delhi: Sterling Publishers Pvt. Ltd (published 2009). pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">3–</span>7. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-207-4352-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-207-4352-6"><bdi>978-81-207-4352-6</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2015-09-05</span></span>. <q>There is a difference between management and manipulation. The difference is thin [...] If management is handling, then manipulation is skillful handling. In short, manipulation is skillful management. [...] Manipulation is in essence leveraged management. [...] It is an alive thing while management is a dead concept. It requires a proactive approach rather than a reactive approach. [...] People cannot be managed.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=People+Manipulation%3A+A+Positive+Approach&amp;rft.place=New+Delhi&amp;rft.pages=3-7&amp;rft.edition=2&amp;rft.pub=Sterling+Publishers+Pvt.+Ltd&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-81-207-4352-6&amp;rft.aulast=Frank&amp;rft.aufirst=Prabbal&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DH9n2vQ7JFVwC&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManagement" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFGilbreth" class="citation book cs1">Gilbreth, Lillian Moller. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/thepsychologyofm16256gut"><i>The Psychology of Management: The Function of the Mind in Determining, Teaching and Installing Methods of Least Waste</i></a> &#8211; via Internet Archive.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Psychology+of+Management%3A+The+Function+of+the+Mind+in+Determining%2C+Teaching+and+Installing+Methods+of+Least+Waste&amp;rft.aulast=Gilbreth&amp;rft.aufirst=Lillian+Moller&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fthepsychologyofm16256gut&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManagement" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFLeggeStantonSmyth2005" class="citation book cs1">Legge, David; Stanton, Pauline; Smyth, Anne (October 2005). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=yA2SR4DgU5wC">"Learning management (and managing your own learning)"</a>. In Harris, Mary G. (ed.). <i>Managing Health Services: Concepts and Practice</i>. Marrickville, NSW: Elsevier Australia (published 2006). p.&#160;13. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7295-3759-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7295-3759-9"><bdi>978-0-7295-3759-9</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2014-07-11</span></span>. <q>The <i>manager as bureaucrat</i> is the guardian of roles, rules, and relationships; his or her style of management relies heavily on working according to the book. In the Weberian tradition, managers are necessary to coordinate the different roles that contribute to the production process and to mediate communication from the head office to the shop floor and back. This style of management assumes a world view in which the bureaucratic role is seen as separate from, and taking precedence over, other constructions of self (including the obligations of citizenship), at least for the working day.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Learning+management+%28and+managing+your+own+learning%29&amp;rft.btitle=Managing+Health+Services%3A+Concepts+and+Practice&amp;rft.place=Marrickville%2C+NSW&amp;rft.pages=13&amp;rft.pub=Elsevier+Australia&amp;rft.date=2005-10&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7295-3759-9&amp;rft.aulast=Legge&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.au=Stanton%2C+Pauline&amp;rft.au=Smyth%2C+Anne&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DyA2SR4DgU5wC&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManagement" class="Z3988"></span></span>
